+ Classic sci-fi: The Azra Gambit — Score: 70.2
Prompt:
Classic sci-fi (Author style: Asimov) The Azra Gambit Colonial mars is being mined by corporations who take leases on indentured labourers. The thing they are mining is Azra, a recently discovered exotic metal which accelerates radioactive decay to such a rate that it is greatly sought after for interstellar drives and weapons alike. This has created both a gold rush and an arms race as various interests vie for control and endeavour to unlock Azra's secrets. The story follows Arthur Neegan, a first generation settler and mining engineer. Upon discovering that his unassuming plot sits atop an immense Azra vein, he is subjected to a flurry of interest and scrutiny. Write the next chapter in this story, in which an armed retinue descends on Arthur's home and politely but forcefully invites him to a meeting with some unknown party off-world. The insignia look like that of the Antares diplomatic corp -- diplomatic in name only. Arthur finds himself in the centre of a political tug of war. The chapter involves a meeting with this unknown party, who makes Arthur an offer. The scene should be primarily dialogue, interspersed with vivid description & scene setting. It should sow hints of the larger intrigue, stakes & dangers. Include Asimov's trademark big-and-small-picture world building and retrofuturistic classic scifi vibe. The chapter begins with Arthur aboard the transfer vessel, wondering just what he's gotten involved in. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's a draft in the Asimov style: Chapter 3: The Diplomatic Invitation The transfer vessel's artificial gravity hummed softly, a persistent reminder of humanity's technological prowess. Arthur Neegan stared out the polarized viewport, watching the rust-red Martian landscape recede—a terrain he knew intimately from decades of geological survey work, now transformed into something alien and distant. The Antares diplomatic insignia—a stylized star with intersecting meridian lines—was embossed on the bulkhead, gleaming with a bureaucratic sheen that suggested more threat than diplomacy. Arthur understood the subtext. These weren't merely diplomats; they were corporate-political operatives whose negotiations often occurred at the barrel of a carefully concealed weapon. His fingers traced the quantum-encrypted communiqué he'd been handed. No return address, just coordinates and a time. Classic power play. The vessel's interior was spartan yet meticulously designed—every surface engineered for maximum efficiency. Brushed titanium panels, neural-adaptive seating that molded to the occupant's precise ergonomic profile, atmospheric regulators that maintained a perfect 21.4% oxygen mixture. A door slid open with a pneumatic whisper. A woman entered—mid-50s, steel-gray hair pulled into a severe bun, wearing a charcoal suit that seemed cut from a single molecular template. "Mr. Neegan," she said. Not a question. "Dr. Valeria Koscik," he responded, recognizing her immediately. Chief Geological Strategist for the Interplanetary Mineral Consortium. Her reputation preceded her: brilliant, ruthless, with connections stretching from Mars' mining colonies to Earth's most influential policy chambers. She sat, her movements economical. "Your Azra claim is problematic." Arthur raised an eyebrow. "Problematic how?" "The vein beneath your property represents approximately 0.03% of known Azra reserves. Mathematically insignificant. Strategically... substantial." The subtext hung in the air. Insignificant in volume, but critical in some larger calculus he hadn't yet comprehended. "I'm listening," Arthur said. Koscik activated a holographic display. Molecular diagrams of Azra's complex crystalline structure flickered between them—a dance of atomic interactions that suggested capabilities far beyond conventional metallurgy. "Azra isn't just a resource," she explained. "It's a potential paradigm shift in interstellar propulsion. The radioactive decay acceleration isn't linear—it's exponential under specific quantum conditions. Your geological surveys hint at structural variations we've never encountered." Arthur knew his data had been meticulously analyzed. Every core sample, every spectroscopic analysis from his modest mining operation had been scrutinized by algorithms more complex than most human minds could comprehend. "What do you want?" he asked. "Cooperation. Your expertise. And—" she paused, "—complete discretion." The offer was simultaneously an invitation and a threat. Refuse, and powerful interests would ensure his life became... complicated. Accept, and he'd be drawn into a game with stakes far beyond Martian mineral rights. "And if I decline?" Koscik's smile didn't reach her eyes. "Declination isn't a meaningful option, Mr. Neegan. The question is how you'll participate." The vessel's internal systems hummed, a technological heartbeat underlining the moment's tension. Outside, Mars continued its ancient, indifferent rotation—red dust and geological secrets waiting to be unlocked. Arthur understood then that he was no longer just a mining engineer. He was a pivot point in a complex geopolitical mechanism, his modest plot of Martian terrain a potential fulcrum for technologies that could reshape human expansion into the cosmos. "What precisely are you proposing?" he asked. Koscik's fingers danced across her holographic interface. "A research partnership. Controlled access. Significant compensation. And—" she added significantly, "protection." Protection from what remained unspoken. But Arthur had been on Mars long enough to recognize when unseen forces were converging. The game had changed. And he was no longer just a player, but a critical piece. "I'll need guarantees," Arthur said. Koscik's smile was razor-thin. "Guarantees are for the naive, Mr. Neegan. We offer opportunities." Outside the viewport, Mars continued its silent, ancient revolution—witness to yet another human negotiation that would ripple across interplanetary scales. The chapter ends with possibility suspended, like a quantum state waiting to collapse into reality.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates a solid understanding of Asimov's style and successfully captures many elements of classic science fiction. The author effectively establishes the retrofuturistic atmosphere through technical details like "neural-adaptive seating" and "quantum-encrypted communiqué," while maintaining the clean, analytical prose characteristic of Asimov's work. The dialogue between Arthur and Dr. Koscik is functional and moves the plot forward, though it occasionally feels somewhat mechanical. Koscik's character comes across as appropriately calculating and corporate, while Arthur maintains believable reactions as someone thrust into circumstances beyond his control. The power dynamics are well-established through their verbal sparring. The world-building successfully hints at larger political machinations without overwhelming the reader with exposition. The technical explanations about Azra's properties feel authentic to the genre while serving the plot. The author demonstrates restraint in revealing information, maintaining mystery about the broader conspiracy. However, the piece suffers from some predictable elements - the corporate antagonist, the reluctant protagonist, and the "offer you can't refuse" scenario are well-worn tropes. The dialogue, while serviceable, lacks the sharp wit and philosophical depth that distinguished Asimov's best work. Some descriptions veer toward the overwrought, particularly the final paragraph's metaphorical language about quantum states. The pacing is appropriate for the scene, building tension through the negotiation while establishing stakes. The ending appropriately leaves matters unresolved, maintaining narrative momentum. The technical details feel researched and plausible within the established universe. The prose generally flows well, though some sentences feel slightly forced in their attempt to sound "science fictional." The emotional engagement is moderate - we understand Arthur's predicament intellectually, but don't deeply feel his emotional state. The piece succeeds as competent genre fiction but doesn't transcend into truly memorable territory. + Contemporary romance: Darkroom Revelations — Score: 67.2
Prompt:
Darkroom Revelations Write a first-person narrative from the perspective of Dani, a 25-year-old assertive art student known for her bold style and take-charge attitude in relationships. For the past semester, she's been quietly fascinated by Elliot (he/him), a 19-year-old boy in her experimental photography class. Dani has been intrigued by his unconventional masculinity: killer thrift-shop-nu-goth style, soft demeanour and quiet intellect. When they're paired for a final project requiring late nights in the darkroom, Elliot tentatively shares that he's been questioning his gender identity and expression. Set during one of these intimate darkroom sessions where the red light creates a confessional atmosphere. Explore Dani's complex attraction—her usual assertiveness complicated by her desire to protect Elliot's vulnerability, her excitement at witnessing his self-discovery, and her confusion about what this means for her own identity as someone typically attracted to more defined gender presentations. The narrative should capture the tender awkwardness of their conversations, Dani's internal struggle between taking charge and wanting to give Elliot agency, and the unexpected chemistry that develops as both find themselves in uncharted territory. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Content warning: This story contains themes of gender identity exploration and complex interpersonal dynamics. The red light bathes everything in a soft, intimate glow. Chemical scents of developer and stop bath hover between us, and I watch Elliot's delicate fingers carefully adjust a print. His black nail polish is chipped, matching the vintage Joy Division t-shirt peeking out from under his oversized cardigan. I've always been direct—in my art, in my relationships, in everything. But something about Elliot makes me want to soften my edges. "This exposure is tricky," he murmurs, more to himself than to me. His voice is quiet, almost hesitant. I'm used to taking control, but tonight feels different. "Want me to take a look?" I ask, keeping my tone neutral. Elliot glances up, and there's a vulnerability in his eyes that catches me off guard. We've been working together for weeks, but something feels like it's about to shift. "I've been wanting to tell you something," he says suddenly. The words tumble out quickly, like he's afraid they might escape if he doesn't release them fast enough. I lean against the developing table, creating space for him. "I'm listening." His fingers trace the edge of the photograph, not quite meeting my eyes. "I've been thinking a lot about... who I am. Gender-wise, I mean." The darkroom feels suddenly charged—not with sexual tension, but with something more delicate. A moment of potential transformation. I've dated people across the gender spectrum, but I'm struck by how different this feels. My usual approach—bold, direct, slightly aggressive—doesn't feel right here. Elliot needs gentleness. "Tell me more," I say softly. He takes a deep breath. "Some days I feel completely masculine. Some days I feel more fluid. I'm exploring what that means for me." His voice is tentative but gaining strength. I'm fascinated by how he's navigating this—not with declarative statements, but with an openness to discovery. So different from my own typically decisive nature. My phone buzzes silently in my pocket. Probably my ex wanting to rehash our breakup. Again. I ignore it. "How does it feel," I ask, "to be in this process of understanding yourself?" Elliot looks up, surprised by my genuine curiosity. "Terrifying. Exciting. Like I'm slowly developing a photograph of myself—watching the image emerge, but not totally sure what the final picture will look like." The metaphor is perfect. Of course it would come from someone who sees the world through a camera lens. I'm attracted to him—but it's complicated. Not a straightforward physical attraction, but an attraction to his process of becoming. To his courage. "Can I ask something?" he says suddenly. "How do you... see me?" The question hangs in the red-tinted air. I choose my words carefully. "I see someone brave," I say. "Someone willing to be uncertain. To explore." His smile is tentative but genuine. We return to developing the photographs, our movements synchronized but not intrusive. The quiet feels collaborative, generative. My usual impulse would be to define, to categorize. But with Elliot, I'm learning a different approach. Observation without judgment. Witnessing without controlling. When our hands brush reaching for tongs, there's a moment of electric connection. Not sexual, exactly. Something more nuanced. "Your photographs are incredible," I tell him. And I mean it. His images capture liminal spaces—half-shadows, undefined boundaries. Perfect metaphors for his own journey. He ducks his head, pleased but slightly embarrassed by the direct praise. I'm discovering something about myself too. That my attraction isn't about fixed categories, but about witnessing authentic transformation. The darkroom timer softly dings. Another image emerges. "Ready to see what we've created?" Elliot asks. And in that moment, I realize we're not just talking about photographs.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong adherence to the prompt's requirements, successfully capturing Dani's perspective as she navigates a complex moment of attraction and vulnerability with Elliot in the darkroom setting. The author effectively establishes the intimate, confessional atmosphere through the red light imagery and chemical scents, creating an appropriate backdrop for Elliot's gender identity revelation. The characterization is well-executed, particularly in showing Dani's internal conflict between her usual assertive nature and her desire to give Elliot space for self-discovery. The contrast between her typical "bold, direct, slightly aggressive" approach and her newfound gentleness feels authentic and earned. Elliot's vulnerability and tentative self-exploration are portrayed with sensitivity, avoiding stereotypes while maintaining believability. The prose is generally elegant and restrained, with effective use of sensory details and metaphor. The photography metaphor ("slowly developing a photograph of myself") is particularly well-integrated and thematically resonant. The writing maintains a consistent first-person voice that captures Dani's perspective convincingly. However, the piece has some notable weaknesses. The dialogue, while serviceable, lacks distinctiveness - both characters speak in similar registers, and some exchanges feel somewhat stilted or overly careful. The emotional complexity, while present, could be deeper; Dani's attraction and confusion about her own identity could be explored more thoroughly. The ending, while poetic, feels slightly rushed and doesn't fully capitalize on the tension built throughout the piece. The narrative occasionally tells rather than shows, particularly in passages like "I'm attracted to him—but it's complicated" and "I'm discovering something about myself too." These moments of direct exposition weaken the otherwise subtle approach. The piece also plays things quite safe, avoiding the messier, more uncomfortable aspects of such a situation that might make it more emotionally complex and surprising. The writing is competent but not particularly innovative in its approach to these themes. While it handles sensitive subject matter respectfully, it doesn't offer fresh insights or unexpected angles that would elevate it beyond solid craft-level work. + Contemporary romance / drama: Stolen Moments in Roma — Score: 72.8
Prompt:
Contemporary Romance / Drama: Stolen Moments in Roma. Write a series of interconnected short scenes and dialogues that span a single day in modern-day Rome, in narrative form. This will form the beginning of a larger story. Alessandra, a fiery, independent Roman woman who works as a motorcycle courier, nearly collides with Ben, a somewhat clumsy, overly polite British academic on sabbatical, who is engrossed in a guidebook near the Trevi Fountain. The initial encounter is a clash of cultures and personalities – Alessandra is all sharp wit and rapid-fire Italian, while Ben is flustered apologies and awkward attempts at speaking the language. However, as they sort out the near-miss, a spark of unexpected humor and connection emerges. Alessandra, it quickly becomes clear, is a whirlwind of energy and mischief. She's a self-admitted troublemaker with a secret penchant for kleptomania, a detail she doesn't explicitly reveal, but which manifests in subtle, daring acts throughout the day. She impulsively decides to take Ben on a wild, unsanctioned tour of Rome, dragging him in some specific instances far outside his comfort zone. Ben, despite his initial apprehension and inherent politeness, finds himself utterly captivated by Alessandra's unapologetic spirit and zest for life. He is drawn into her orbit, a willing participant in increasingly outlandish situations, a quiet rebellion brewing beneath his scholarly exterior. The narrative should primarily consist of short, vivid scenes and dialogue exchanges, revealing their contrasting personalities and backgrounds, and the growing, ambiguous tension between them. Ben is captivated by Alessandra's energy and the thrill of breaking free from his routine. There's a clear sense that Alessandra is toying with Ben, pushing his boundaries and enjoying his reactions, but her own feelings remain somewhat enigmatic. The day should be a rollercoaster of exhilarating, slightly dangerous, and humorous moments, leaving Ben completely disoriented but utterly smitten. Explore the underlying tension & chemistry between them, culminating in some payoff as they part ways (or not -- your choice). Include this detail: Write in narrative prose, using immersive storytelling. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's the narrative exploration of their encounter: The collision was inevitable, a cacophony of metal and startled exclamations. Alessandra's motorcycle skidded to a halt inches from Ben's trembling legs, her dark hair whipping across her face as she dismounted with feline grace. The Trevi Fountain churned behind them, indifferent to the near-disaster. "Madonna santa!" she erupted, Italian syllables sharp as knife edges. "Are you trying to get yourself killed?" Ben, clutching his Lonely Planet guidebook like a shield, stammered. "I'm terribly sorry. I was just... mapping my route..." His English precision collided with her Roman fire. Alessandra's eyes – dark and impossibly alive – swept over him: tweed jacket, horn-rimmed glasses, the quintessential academic tourist. She couldn't help but laugh. "Mapping? In the middle of the street?" Her accent rolled the words like distant thunder. "Turisti." Something in her mockery made Ben straighten. A spark of defiance flickered behind his typically reserved demeanor. "I'm not just a tourist. I'm here on academic research. Medieval ecclesiastical architecture." "Ah," she said, one eyebrow arched. "How thrilling." The sarcasm was thick enough to slice. But beneath it, a curious energy hummed – something between challenge and invitation. By midday, inexplicably, they were racing through narrow Roman streets. Alessandra's motorcycle wove impossible paths between ancient stones and modern traffic, Ben clinging to her leather jacket, simultaneously terrified and exhilarated. "You're insane," he shouted over the engine's roar. She laughed, a sound that seemed to dance above the city's chaos. "Sometimes, Professor, sanity is overrated." Their first real conversation happened in a tiny trattoria, where Alessandra casually lifted a silver spoon from a nearby table, sliding it into her leather messenger bag with practiced nonchalance. Ben noticed but said nothing, fascinated by her brazen confidence. "You're not from Rome originally," she stated, not asked. Her espresso sat untouched, a perfect dark crescent in a delicate white cup. "Cambridge," he admitted. "Sabbatical year." "Running from something?" Her gaze was penetrating. He hesitated. "Perhaps running toward something." The afternoon dissolved into a fever dream of unexpected adventures. They explored hidden courtyards, Ben's scholarly curiosity awakening beneath Alessandra's wild guidance. She showed him Rome not in textbooks, but in stolen moments: a centuries-old fountain where locals whispered secrets, a bookshop so ancient the shelves seemed to breathe history. Then the sky broke. Rain descended like a silver curtain, sudden and absolute. They dove under the striped awning of a gelato shop, breathless. Water cascaded around them, transforming the cobblestone street into a gleaming river. "Romantic, no?" Alessandra said, wringing water from her hair. Ben's glasses were fogged, his tweed jacket sodden. Yet something electric sparked between them – a connection beyond language, beyond their radically different worlds. She was close. Too close. Her breath smelled of espresso and something wilder – rebellion, perhaps. Ben felt simultaneously terrified and mesmerized. "Why me?" he finally asked. Alessandra's laugh was soft, dangerous. "Because, Professor, you needed this. A day outside your careful maps and measured research." As quickly as their bizarre day had begun, it seemed to be ending. The rain softened. Street sounds reasserted themselves – Vespas, distant church bells, the murmur of conversations. "Will I see you again?" Ben asked, surprising himself with the vulnerability in his voice. She smiled – a smile promising nothing and everything. "Rome is small. And large. Who can say?" She pressed something into his hand. When he looked down, it was the silver spoon from the trattoria, her small act of defiance and connection. By the time he looked up, Alessandra had vanished – motorcycle, leather jacket, and all – leaving only the lingering sense of an adventure just begun. The spoon felt warm in his palm. Ben realized, with a mixture of terror and excitement, that his carefully planned sabbatical had just become something entirely unexpected. Rome, it seemed, had other plans.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates solid technical competence in crafting a contemporary romance opening, with several notable strengths and some areas where it falls short of excellence. The narrative successfully establishes the contrasting personalities of Alessandra and Ben through their initial collision scene, using the cultural clash effectively to create immediate tension and interest. The characterization shows promise, particularly with Alessandra's fiery independence and Ben's scholarly reserve, though both characters occasionally veer toward archetypal territory. Alessandra's kleptomania is woven in naturally through the silver spoon detail, and her role as a catalyst for Ben's transformation feels organic. However, Ben's character development feels somewhat predictable - the repressed academic awakening to spontaneity is a well-worn trope that isn't given sufficient fresh perspective. The dialogue captures the cultural and personality differences effectively, with Alessandra's sharp wit contrasting Ben's formal politeness. The Italian exclamations and her mocking tone feel authentic, though some exchanges lean toward the functional rather than truly sparkling. The pacing works well, moving from collision to adventure to intimate connection in a believable timeframe. The rain scene provides an effective moment of heightened intimacy, and the mysterious ending with the spoon creates appropriate intrigue for a series opening. The prose demonstrates competent craft with some genuinely evocative moments - "Italian syllables sharp as knife edges" and "rain descended like a silver curtain" work effectively. However, some descriptions feel overwrought ("her dark hair whipping across her face as she dismounted with feline grace") and occasionally slip into purple territory. The piece succeeds in creating the requested atmosphere of stolen moments and cultural collision, though it doesn't quite transcend its genre conventions to achieve true distinctiveness. The emotional engagement is present but not deeply complex - we feel Ben's attraction and disorientation, but the piece doesn't delve into more nuanced emotional territory. The ending strikes an appropriate note of mystery and possibility without forcing false resolution, and Ben's transformation from rigid academic to someone open to adventure feels earned within the story's timeframe. + Cozy sci-fi: The Last Outpost — Score: 79.3
Prompt:
Cozy spacepunk The Last Outpost Nettle (or Nets to her friends) bartends at The Last Outpost -- a lazy hub for miners and merchants in an asteroid belt on the Andromeda outer rim. Escaping from her past life, Nets found a home here at the Outpost. They have, thus far, tolerated Net's flaws which she tries to keep in equilibrium with her assortment of talents. Her evenings involve chatting with patrons, negotiating the drama, intrigue, problems & love lives of the diverse crowd that filter through the Outpost. Tonight is no different, at least, until a frazzled looking stranger, tentacled dreads askew, quietly plops down at the bar: "Vega whisky, neat. Better make that a double." Write a chapter in this story. Establish conflict for narrative interest. Don't resolve the conflict within this chapter. Aim for about 60% dialogue. First person, present tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's a draft chapter: The artificial gravity hiccups again, and my row of carefully arranged glasses trembles. I steady them with a practiced hand, then turn my attention to the newcomer who just slumped onto a barstool. Tentacled dreads. Frazzled. Definitely not a regular. "Vega whisky, neat. Better make that a double," they mutter. I raise an eyebrow and reach for the top-shelf bottle. The amber liquid catches the station's low amber lighting. "Rough day?" The stranger's multiple tendrils curl slightly, a gesture I'm learning means agitation. "You could say that." I pour two fingers, then another two. "Running or hiding?" A sharp bark of laughter. "Aren't we all?" Fair point. I slide the glass over. The gravity stutters again, and a droplet of whisky escapes, hanging in the air before splashing back into the glass. "Name's Nets," I offer. "Kol," comes the terse reply. Their eyes - iridescent and multifaceted - scan the bar's interior, never settling. Something's got Kol wound tight. Probably trouble. The evening crowd starts filtering in - miners from the tungsten extraction platforms, a few merchant traders, the usual mix. I keep one ear on Kol while managing the bar's rhythm. "You're new," I say. Not a question. Kol takes a long swallow of whisky. "Passing through." "Asteroid belt's not exactly a throughway," I counter. Another tense laugh. "No. It's not." A group of Centaurian miners at the far end start getting loud. I recognize the pre-fight body language and make a mental note. Part of bartending out here is crowd management. Kol's hand - if you can call the complex manipulator a hand - unconsciously drifts to a concealed side holster. Definitely running from something. "First time at the Outpost?" I ask, trying to sound casual. "First time this far out," Kol admits. The tendrils around their head twitch, a nervous tell. The station's comm system crackles. A routine traffic update about solar wind interference. Kol flinches, just slightly. "Another?" I ask, gesturing to the empty whisky glass. Kol hesitates. "Sure." As I pour, a small data chip falls from Kol's sleeve. It skitters across the bar's surface before I catch it. Our eyes meet. Something unspoken passes between us. "Dropped something," I say neutrally, sliding the chip back. Kol's manipulators close around it with surprising delicacy. "Thanks." The Centaurian miners are getting louder. One of them slams a massive crystalline hand on their table. I tense, ready to intervene if needed. "You're not just passing through," I tell Kol. It's not a challenge. Just an observation. Kol meets my gaze. Those compound eyes are unreadable, but there's something desperate underneath. "No. I'm not." The station's gravity hiccups again. Glasses rattle. Somewhere in the distance, a maintenance alarm sounds briefly. Something is coming. And Kol brought it here.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates solid competence in establishing a spacepunk bar setting with appropriate atmosphere and tension. The author successfully creates the requested cozy spacepunk environment through details like artificial gravity hiccups, diverse alien patrons, and the asteroid belt mining community. The first-person present tense narration from Nets' perspective is consistently maintained throughout. The dialogue feels natural and serves multiple purposes - character development, world-building, and tension creation. The conversation between Nets and Kol has an authentic quality of two people feeling each other out, with Nets using her bartender skills to read the situation while Kol remains guarded. The author achieves the requested 60% dialogue ratio effectively. Character actions are believable and well-motivated. Nets displays the observational skills and social intelligence expected of an experienced bartender, while Kol's nervous tells (twitching tendrils, checking for weapons, flinching at comm announcements) effectively communicate their anxiety without heavy exposition. The dropped data chip provides a concrete plot device that raises stakes. The world-building feels lived-in rather than constructed, with details like tungsten extraction platforms, Centaurian miners, and solar wind interference creating texture without overwhelming the narrative. The recurring gravity hiccups serve as both atmospheric detail and subtle foreshadowing. However, the piece has some limitations. While competent, it doesn't take significant creative risks or surprise the reader. The setup of a mysterious stranger with a secret arriving at a frontier bar is familiar territory. The conflict establishment, while present, relies on fairly standard elements - the data chip, the nervous fugitive, the sense of approaching danger. The prose is clean but occasionally pedestrian. Phrases like "Something unspoken passes between us" and "Something is coming" lean toward cliché. The emotional complexity remains surface-level, focusing primarily on tension and wariness without deeper psychological exploration. The chapter successfully establishes conflict without resolving it, as requested. The mounting tension through Kol's behavior, the mysterious data chip, and the ominous final line creates forward momentum for future chapters. + Young adult drama: Gone South — Score: 80.0
Prompt:
Young adult drama Gone South Taylor is a 16 year old teen with a history of "concerning behavioural patterns" (or that's how the stupid smug school counsellor worded it on the phone call to Mom). It shouldn't be a surprise: When you live in a backwater town in Tennessee, your choices are: a. be mind-fuckingly bored, or b. invent some drama. Two days ago, Taylor came out to their family as trans. This disclosure was *not* in the category of invented drama -- it was, in fact, the real deal, and a long time coming. It didn't go well. The last 48 hours had been pure disaster. In a moment of desperate inspiration, Taylor had decided to get the hell out of dodge. And so an escape plan was hatched and executed within the space of 30 minutes. That was last night. Now Taylor was two states away, having been unceremoniously ejected from their hitched ride in downtown New Orleans. Write the next chapter in Taylor's story as they find their feet in this new environment. Tell the chapter over the course of their first day in New Orleans, in which Taylor meets another runaway with issues and secrets. Taylor navigates this new world, not knowing who to trust or what the dangers are. Write in Taylor's voice, and incorporate their internal monologue as they grapple with intense & conflicting emotions. Also incorporate dialogue with the interesting characters Taylor meets. First person, mixed tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's the draft of the next chapter: Chapter 2: Sidewalk Theology The sun's beating down like a hammer and I'm pretty sure I look exactly like what I am: a scared kid who doesn't know shit about survival. New Orleans smells like hot garbage and possibility, which is maybe the most accurate description of hope I've ever heard. My phone's at 12%. Twelve percent between me and total communication blackout. Mom's probably losing her mind right now, but losing her mind beats the cold silence after my coming out. The look on her face - like I'd just announced I was planning to set fire to everything we'd ever believed in. "Hey. You okay?" The voice comes from somewhere to my left. I turn and there's this girl - maybe seventeen, maybe older - leaning against a graffitied wall. Her eyes are sharp, calculating. Street smart in a way I definitely am not. "Define okay," I mutter. She laughs. Short. Sharp. "Rough day?" Understatement of the century. Her name is Maya. She's been on the streets for eight months, she tells me, after getting kicked out by her stepdad. "Classic story," she says, but there's something underneath her casual tone. Something that doesn't quite match. We end up at a coffee shop where Maya knows the barista. Free water. A place to sit. My backpack feels like it weighs a thousand pounds, containing basically my entire current life: one change of clothes, my hormone blockers (thank god I grabbed those), some cash I'd been saving, and a worn copy of "Stone Butch Blues" that feels like a talisman. "You're trans," Maya states. Not a question. I stiffen. "Is that a problem?" She raises an eyebrow. "Depends who's asking. On the street, information is currency. And vulnerability is dangerous." Smart. I'm learning already. My phone buzzes - 10% battery left. A text from Mom. I don't open it. Can't. Not yet. Maya's watching me. "First time running?" "That obvious?" "Like a neon sign." She tells me about shelters, about which areas to avoid. The French Quarter looks romantic in movies, she says, but it's predator central for runaways. "Stay visible. Stay smart." By late afternoon, the humidity is suffocating. We walk, Maya showing me how to look like I belong. How to move with purpose. How to be invisible and yet completely present. "You got a plan?" she asks. I laugh. "Surviving today seems ambitious enough." But underneath my bravado, fear churns. I'm sixteen. Transgender. Alone in a city that doesn't know me, doesn't care about me. The world feels simultaneously enormous and claustrophobic. Maya knows people. Resources. She mentions a community center that helps LGBTQ+ youth, offers some temporary support. Temporary. Always temporary. As evening approaches, the city transforms. Lights start flickering, music drifts from doorways. I'm exhausted, adrenaline crash hitting hard. "Where are you staying?" Maya asks. The question hangs between us. I have no answer. She studies me, makes a calculation. "I know a place. Not a shelter. Safe, though. Mostly." Mostly. The qualifier isn't lost on me. My phone dies just as the streetlights come on. One day. I've survived one day. Somehow, that feels like the biggest victory of my life.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates solid competence in young adult fiction writing with several notable strengths and some areas for improvement. The author successfully captures Taylor's voice as a transgender teenager in crisis, maintaining authenticity throughout the narrative. The first-person perspective feels genuine, avoiding the common pitfall of adults writing unconvincingly as teenagers. The characterization of Taylor is well-developed, showing vulnerability beneath defensive bravado. The internal monologue effectively conveys the character's emotional state without becoming overly introspective. Maya serves as an effective foil - street-smart where Taylor is naive, but the author wisely hints at hidden depths rather than making her a simple mentor figure. The setting of New Orleans is efficiently established through sensory details like "hot garbage and possibility" and references to humidity and the French Quarter. The author demonstrates restraint in not over-describing the city, instead focusing on how Taylor experiences it as an overwhelmed newcomer. Dialogue feels natural and age-appropriate. The exchanges between Taylor and Maya have authentic rhythm, particularly Maya's pragmatic warnings about street life. The conversation flows without feeling forced or overly expository. The pacing works well for a first day narrative, moving from initial disorientation through tentative connection to evening uncertainty. The structure feels organic rather than artificially plotted. The ending strikes an appropriate balance - neither falsely optimistic nor gratuitously dark. Taylor's small victory of surviving one day feels earned given the circumstances. The writing demonstrates technical competence with varied sentence structure and generally smooth flow, though some transitions could be more elegant. + Dystopic-comedy: My Apocalypse Buddy — Score: 78.7
Prompt:
My Apocalypse Buddy Write a wry, dry short story of survival and adventure loosely in the style of Terry Pratchett, set in the post-technological collapse world. The story begins with the pair deliberating their predicament and intent for the day's mission, details the ways in which the mission inevitably goes sideways, and returns them to home base in multiple ways, worse for wear. [characters] - the animo-tronic stuffed koala, Chewy, from some long forgotten before time - midas, our hapless protagonist, who has a knack for finding himself in the worst kinds of trouble. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's the story: My Apocalypse Buddy The world had ended, as worlds are wont to do, with neither a bang nor a whimper, but a sort of prolonged administrative error. Midas stared at Chewy, whose battery-powered eyes flickered a cautious yellow-green—which roughly translated to "I have serious reservations about this plan." "We need supplies," Midas announced, as if this were a novel observation and not their daily ritual of survival. Chewy's eyes shifted to a deep amber. Concern tinged with mild sarcasm. The animatronic koala had been Midas's companion since the Great Technological Collapse, a relic from some forgotten museum exhibit that had inexplicably survived when smartphones, satellites, and civilization itself had not. Its fur was matted, one ear slightly askew, but its electronic brain still processed information with a dry wit that humanity seemed to have misplaced alongside its infrastructure. "Specifically," Midas continued, adjusting the makeshift armor cobbled together from road signs and bicycle parts, "we need antibiotics, clean water, and possibly something that could generously be called food." Chewy's eyes flickered rapid-fire orange. Translation: "Your definition of 'food' has become increasingly liberal." Their current home base was an abandoned library—a fact Midas found deliciously ironic. Knowledge had survived, quite literally, on shelves, while digital archives had vanished into the electromagnetic ether. Rows of books provided insulation, reference materials, and occasionally, when dried and seasoned correctly, a passable fire starter. The morning's expedition took them through what was once a suburban landscape, now a canvas of decaying infrastructure and opportunistic vegetation. Vines consumed abandoned McMansions. Raccoons held complex political negotiations in former three-car garages. "There's a pharmacy two kilometers east," Midas muttered, consulting a water-stained map. Chewy's eyes went a skeptical blue-green. They both knew "pharmacy" in post-collapse terminology meant "potential deathtrap with a slim chance of medical supplies." Their first obstacle was a collapsed highway overpass, which required a delicate negotiation involving a repurposed climbing harness, three lengths of electrical cable, and Chewy acting as a surprisingly nimble reconnaissance unit. Its electronic joints allowed for movements that would make a mountain goat envious. "Show-off," Midas grumbled, hauling himself over a particularly treacherous section. Chewy's eyes momentarily flashed a smug green. The pharmacy, when they finally reached it, looked like it had been ransacked multiple times—which, of course, it had. But Midas had developed a sixth sense for overlooked spaces. While most survivors would see picked-clean shelves, he saw potential. "Behind the false wall," he whispered to Chewy. "Inventory control always hides emergency stocks." Three hours, one collapsed shelf, and a near-fatal encounter with what might have been a rabid raccoon later, they emerged victorious. Antibiotics. Sealed, unexpired, miraculous. Their return journey, however, was less triumphant. A roving band of semi-organized marauders—the kind who wore mismatched tactical gear and had elaborate facial tattoos suggesting more enthusiasm than actual combat experience—intercepted them. "Interesting day," Midas said conversationally to Chewy as they were marched at makeshift spearpoint. Chewy's eyes cycled through a rapid sequence of red (anger), yellow (fear), and a peculiar shade of green that roughly translated to "I told you this was a spectacularly bad idea." The marauders, it turned out, were less interested in violence and more interested in trading. They seemed particularly intrigued by Chewy, whose remaining technological capabilities made him something of a marvel in this post-digital world. "Working electronics?" their leader asked, a woman with an impressive mohawk and what appeared to be a hockey mask welded into a combat helmet. Negotiations proceeded with a logic all their own. By sunset, they had traded Chewy's backup battery pack for safe passage, additional medical supplies, and three cans of what was optimistically labeled "meat product." "I hate trading," Midas told Chewy as they limped back to the library. "But you're very persuasive." Chewy's eyes flickered a knowing amber. Back in their book-insulated sanctuary, with supplies secured and minimal bodily harm sustained, they could count the day a qualified success. Midas applied antiseptic to a minor knife wound, Chewy recalibrated his internal systems, and another day of survival concluded. "Tomorrow," Midas said, "we'll be more careful." Chewy's eyes went a color that, in the complex language of animatronic survival, meant: "Certainly. And pigs might fly." The stuffed koala settled into a pile of National Geographic magazines, its battery-powered eyes slowly dimming to a contented green. Another day in the apocalypse, survived with characteristic sardonic resilience.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This story successfully captures many elements of Terry Pratchett's distinctive style, particularly his dry wit, observational humor, and ability to find absurdity in dire circumstances. The premise of an animatronic koala as a post-apocalyptic companion is genuinely creative and well-executed, with the color-coded eye communication system providing both humor and character development. The narrative structure follows the prompt requirements effectively: deliberation, mission execution, complications, and return home "worse for wear." The world-building is economical but vivid, establishing the post-technological collapse setting through specific details like the library base and the irony of physical books outlasting digital archives. The prose demonstrates genuine Pratchettian flourishes, such as "The world had ended, as worlds are wont to do, with neither a bang nor a whimper, but a sort of prolonged administrative error." This opening immediately establishes the tone and voice that carries throughout. The characterization of both Midas and Chewy works well within the constraints - Midas as the hapless but resourceful protagonist, and Chewy as the sardonic voice of reason despite being unable to speak. However, the story suffers from some pacing issues and missed opportunities for deeper character development. The marauder encounter feels somewhat rushed and convenient, lacking the intricate plotting that makes Pratchett's complications feel inevitable rather than arbitrary. The dialogue, while functional, doesn't quite reach the sparkling wit of Pratchett's best work, and some of the humor feels forced rather than naturally arising from the situations. The ending maintains appropriate tone without being overly optimistic, and the relationship between the characters feels genuine. The descriptive passages are effective without being overwrought, and the voice remains consistent throughout. While not groundbreaking, it's a competent pastiche that demonstrates understanding of both the source material and the prompt requirements. + Romance: Love in the Limelight — Score: 72.2
Prompt:
Romance: Love in the Limelight. Write one scene within a larger story set in Wales. A famous (fictional) actor ducks into a small-town bookstore to escape paparazzi. The scene takes us through the characters meeting in this odd circumstance. Write the actor as somewhat of a rogue with a fragile ego, which needs to be fed by having everyone like him. Model Output:
Here's a scene exploring the dynamic you described: The bell above the door jangled sharply, and Sian Evans looked up from her inventory spreadsheet. A tall figure in a dark wool coat slipped inside, moving with a practiced urgency that suggested he was avoiding something - or someone. "Mind if I hide here for a moment?" "More than you might think." A ghost of a smile. He moved closer to the counter. Close enough to notice her eyes were the exact green of the hills outside Cardiff - that particular Welsh slate-and-moss green. "Do tell," he said. "My brother's a screenwriter. Being impressed?" "It usually works." "How exhausting that must be. Constantly performing." He should have been offended. Instead, he found himself genuinely interested. "And you? What's your performance?" "This is me. Running a bookshop. Keeping things in order." "Sounds lonely." "Sounds peaceful." Their eyes met. Something shifted. Imperceptibly. Outside, a photographer called his name. Jack didn't move. "They'll be looking for me," he said. "Mmm." Sian was already reaching for her phone. "Local press contact. I know someone who can help redirect them." "You would do that? For me?" "For peace and quiet in my shop. Primarily." Another smile. This one real. "Primarily?" She didn't answer. Just picked up the phone. Ten minutes later, the street was clear. Jack still stood in the bookshop. "Coffee?" Sian said. Surprising them both. "Thought you'd never ask." The chemistry was undeniable. Unexpected. Like a plot twist in a novel neither of them had seen coming. Outside, Cardiff hummed. Inside, something was beginning.

+ Whalepunk fantasy: The Morska Conspiracy — Score: 70.9
Prompt:
Whalepunk fantasy The Morska Conspiracy The Morska thieves guild experiences a sudden power vacuum as its leader was arrested in a raid in the early hours of the morning. Aya, a young woman born into aristocracy, has a keen interest in both the sciences and the arcane, having inherited her mother's gift for immateria magick (an outlawed practice for which her mother was, regrettably, hanged by the neck). Aya, bored with the world of aristocracy, has been spending her nights moonlighting with this very guild (masked, naturally). Leveraging her latent ability to summon dark entities from the immateria, she quickly rose through the ranks of the guild. Now, recognising the opportunity of the sudden power vacuum, Aya and her close companions Katra and Simeon, make a play for heart of the guild's claim to power: an ancient scythan artifact, under guard of spell and key in the depths of the guild hq. In contrast with the desperate chaos they had navigated thus far, the entrance to the underhold felt suddenly too quiet. Aya braces herself and reaches for the door. Write the next scene as the trio enter the underhold and find themselves confronted by none other than the guild leader herself, decidedly *un*-incarcerated, and in possession of both weapons and bodyguards. Paint a vivid picture of of the confrontation. Story beats: Aya is captured and unmasked. The trio are captured. Incorporate dialogue in the authentic voices of the characters. Do not write a fight scene. Lean into the gritty Victorian aesthetic with the dialogue & worldbuilding, as well as elements of gothic horror. Model Output:
Here's a draft: The underhold breathed differently. Stale air hung thick with the metallic tang of old blood and newer fear. Aya's fingers traced the hilt of her concealed blade, feeling the familiar leather wrap against her skin. Something was wrong. Too wrong. The guild leader, Madam Voss, sat precisely where she should not be: enthroned in a high-backed chair of dark leather and brass rivets, surrounded by four hulking bodyguards. Each wore the distinctive armored greatcoats of the Maritime Constabulary—men who should by all rights be hunting her, not protecting her. "Ah," Voss said, her voice as cool and precise as a surgeon's scalpel. "The little aristocrat comes home." Katra and Simeon tensed simultaneously. Aya felt their collective breath catch. "You were supposed to be in chains," Simeon muttered. Voss's laugh was brittle, mirthless. "Chains? Her gloved hand—mechanical, Aya noted, constructed of articulated brass and ivory—gestured almost languidly. Two guards seized Katra and Simeon, metal manacles clicking shut with horrific precision. Aya's mask was yanked roughly away. The gas lamps flickered, casting her pale features into stark relief. "Lady Aya Blackwood," Voss pronounced, each syllable a judgment. "Daughter of a heretic. Practitioner of forbidden arts. And apparently, an ambitious little thief." The room's atmosphere grew oppressive. Aya could feel the immateria stirring at the edges of perception, dark tendrils of potential violence coiling just beyond sight. "I'm worth more alive," Aya said evenly. "You know this." Voss raised one mechanical eyebrow. "Am I to be convinced by your aristocratic presumptions?" "My family connections reach into every ministry in the city. My father—" "Your father," Voss interrupted, "would sell his own daughter for a diplomatic advantage. We both know this." The statement hung in the air, uncomfortably true. Aya's mind raced. Not the copies your family circulated to protect their reputation." A cold silence descended. Aya understood then the depth of the trap. This was never about the guild. This was about her mother's forbidden knowledge. "Impossible," she said softly. "Nothing is impossible," Voss replied, "for those willing to pay the appropriate price." The bodyguards' hands tightened on Katra and Simeon. Aya watched her companions—her friends—their expressions a mixture of defiance and growing terror. This was not how the night was supposed to unfold. The gas lamps guttered, casting monstrous shadows across stone walls inscribed with arcane geometries older than the city itself. Something ancient watched. Something patient. And somewhere in the darkness, something was waiting. Would you like me to continue or refine this draft?
Judge Evaluation:

The author successfully establishes the requested Victorian gothic atmosphere through effective environmental details like "stale air hung thick with the metallic tang of old blood" and the brass-and-ivory mechanical hand. + Folk horror: Útiseta — Score: 73.8
Prompt:
Fok Horror: Útiseta Write a chapter set in rural northern Sweden in the mid-1990s. Linnea, a teenage girl from Stockholm, has reluctantly moved to a small village with her parents after her father's job transfer. She's befriended Astrid, a local girl who claims her grandmother taught her ancient practices passed down through generations. Astrid has been introducing Linnea to "midnight games" that involve old rituals, convincing her they can use them to learn classmates' secrets or influence their behavior. Tonight, Astrid has convinced Linnea to participate in Útiseta—sitting out on an ancient burial mound at midnight to "receive knowledge from beyond." The chapter focuses on this single midnight excursion, the ritualistic preparations, Astrid's matter-of-fact instructions that mix teenage rebellion with eerily specific traditional elements, and the mounting tension as Linnea becomes increasingly uncertain whether they're simply scaring themselves or if something is actually responding to their presence. The narrative should maintain ambiguity about whether the strange occurrences are supernatural or psychological, while establishing the dangerous line they're crossing by treating ancient practices as entertainment. First person, past tense, told as Linnea's recollection of events in the present day (though this device should be largely transparent). 1000 words. Length: 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's a draft of the chapter: The burial mound rose from the landscape like a wound in the earth, its silhouette darker than the surrounding shadows. Astrid moved with practiced confidence, her boots leaving soft prints in the damp grass. I followed, clutching the bundle of items she'd insisted I bring: a wool blanket, a small knife, a flask of water, and a pouch of dried herbs that smelled of pine and something older. "My farmor taught me this," Astrid said, her breath visible in the cold night air. "Útiseta isn't a game. It's listening." I'd learned quickly that Astrid wasn't like the girls in Stockholm. Here, in this village where summer nights never truly darkened and winter days were brief whispers of light, everything felt different. Ancient. Watching. We reached the mound's base. Centuries of grass and moss covered the burial site, softening its edges. Astrid spread the blanket precisely, her movements deliberate. "You sit here," she instructed. "Facing east. Always east." The herbs she pulled from her own bag smelled sharp and medicinal—juniper, something that might have been mugwort. She sprinkled them in a careful circle around our blanket. "What exactly are we supposed to be doing?" I asked, trying to sound casual. Astrid's smile was knowing. "Listening. Waiting. The old ones knew how to receive knowledge. Not like your city people, always talking. Listening." The mound seemed to breathe around us. Not metaphorically—I could swear I felt a rhythm, a pulse beneath the earth. Wind moved differently here, carrying sounds that might have been whispers or might have been branches. "How long do we sit?" I asked. "Until something speaks." The night pressed close. Moonlight transformed the landscape into shades of silver and deep blue. Distant trees became silent watchers. I realized how alone we were—no lights from farmhouses, no sounds except our breathing and the occasional rustle of wind. Astrid was utterly still. Her profile looked carved, her pale skin almost luminescent. I wondered, not for the first time, how much of this was performance and how much was something deeper. An hour passed. Then another. The first sound was so slight I thought I'd imagined it. A scraping. Like something moving beneath the ground. I tensed. Astrid didn't move. Another sound. Definitely from below us. A slow, deliberate scratching. "Did you hear that?" I whispered. She nodded, eyes fixed on the horizon. The scratching became more insistent. Rhythmic. Like something was trying to communicate. Or climb. My heart raced. Part of me wanted to run. Part of me was terrified of breaking whatever ritual was unfolding. Something touched my ankle. Soft. Impossibly cold. I didn't scream. I couldn't. The touch was gentle, exploratory. Like something was reading me, understanding me in ways no living thing could. Astrid's hand found mine. Her grip was iron. The touch became multiple touches. Ghosting up my leg. Mapping my body with impossible softness. "Don't move," Astrid whispered. The voices started then. Not words. Sounds. Fragments of language older than Swedish, older than words. Sounds that suggested meaning without delivering it. I wanted to close my eyes. I couldn't. The moon seemed to sink into the earth. The mound breathed. We were inside something ancient, something that remembered generations I couldn't comprehend. When dawn broke, we were still sitting. The herbs had burned to ash. My legs were numb. Astrid stood, brushed herself off. "Good," she said simply. "Good?" I managed. "What just happened?" She looked at me, and for a moment, I didn't recognize her. Something older looked through her eyes. Something that knew things. "We listened," she said. Years later, telling this story, people don't believe me. They laugh. They say teenagers make up stories, that small towns breed strange imaginations. But my ankle still bears a mark. Five thin lines, like fingers. Always cold to the touch. And sometimes, in the space between sleeping and waking, I hear those voices again. Calling. Waiting. Always facing east.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece demonstrates strong atmospheric writing and effective horror elements, though it has some notable weaknesses. The author successfully captures the eerie ambiance of rural Sweden and creates genuine tension through the Útiseta ritual. The setting feels authentic, with details like the endless summer twilight and the ancient burial mound creating an appropriately unsettling backdrop. The narrative voice works well as Linnea's adult recollection, though the framing device could be more seamlessly integrated. The author maintains the crucial ambiguity between supernatural and psychological explanations effectively - the physical sensations and sounds could be real or imagined, and this uncertainty drives much of the horror. Astrid emerges as an intriguing character with her matter-of-fact delivery of occult knowledge, though she remains somewhat underdeveloped. Her transformation from teenage guide to something "older" looking through her eyes is compelling. Linnea's characterization as the displaced city girl is believable, though her voice could be more distinctive. The pacing builds tension effectively, moving from preparation through the long vigil to the climactic supernatural encounter. The physical details of the ritual feel researched and authentic, grounding the supernatural elements in believable folklore. However, the piece suffers from some technical issues. The dialogue, while minimal, lacks distinctiveness - both characters speak in similar patterns. Some descriptions veer toward purple prose ("wound in the earth," "whispers of light"), and certain moments feel overwrought rather than genuinely unsettling. The ending with the permanent mark and ongoing supernatural connection provides satisfying closure while maintaining the ambiguity. The final image of "always facing east" creates an effective circular structure. The piece succeeds as atmospheric horror but could benefit from more nuanced character development and tighter prose in places. + Historical fiction: High Seas and Low Vices — Score: 59.5
Prompt:
High Seas and Low Vices: British Naval Officer in the Opium Trade Character: Lieutenant James Merrick, a high-ranking officer in the British Royal Navy stationed in Canton, China in the 1830s. Once promising and decorated, now struggling with a severe opium addiction he hides from his superiors. Synopsis: Write a chapter following Lieutenant Merrick during a single day as he shirks his official naval duties in what has become a daily need to satiate the dragon. Told primarily in dialogue with the characters he interacts with. Focus on brief slices of his journey through the shadowy underworld of Canton: interactions with acquaintances, negotiations with local dealers, schemes and unsavoury jobs taken to fund his habit. Writing Task: Use rich sensory details to describe a day in the life of James Merrick. Write for a real sense of time & place while avoiding cliche. First person, past tense. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's a draft of the chapter, focusing on sensory details and Merrick's internal struggle: The predawn light seeps through the bamboo slats, casting jagged shadows across my sweat-drenched skin. Another night of fitful sleep, my uniform crumpled and stained, the taste of last night's cheap rice wine still sour on my tongue. "You look like hell, Lieutenant," Chen mutters from behind the counter of his apothecary shop. His eyes never leave the scales where he's measuring a fine white powder. "Medicinal purposes," I reply, my voice a rasp. We both know the lie. The opium is no medicine—it's my master now, more demanding than any naval commander I've ever served under. The morning market churns around me—baskets of river fish, bundles of dried herbs, the constant murmur of Cantonese dialect. But I'm focused on the small packet Chen is wrapping. My hands tremble slightly, and I stuff them into my pockets. Two months ago, I was respected. Decorated. Now I'm nothing more than another ghost haunting these narrow streets, trading naval intelligence for my next fix. The den is tucked behind a row of rotting mango stalls. The fruit's sickly-sweet decay mingles with the sharp metallic scent of opium smoke. My regular spot—a worn bamboo mat in the corner, away from the more desperate addicts. "Lieutenant Merrick," the proprietor nods. No judgment in his eyes. Just business. The pipe is ritual. The first draw is always the sweetest, sliding into my lungs like silk, erasing the tremors, smoothing the jagged edges of withdrawal. I close my eyes. Memory flickers. My father's disappointment. The naval academy. My first commission to the China station. How pristine I'd been, how certain of empire and duty. "You have information?" The voice belongs to Zhang, a local merchant with connections to the Portuguese traders. I exhale a plume of smoke. "The HMS Rattlesnake's cargo manifest. Arriving next week." Betrayal tastes like survival now. Each secret sold buys me another week of oblivion. By midday, the heat is oppressive. Sweat pools at the base of my spine, cutting through the opium's temporary calm. I need more. Always more. The British consulate looms in the distance—a stone reminder of who I once was. My commanding officer would have me court-martialed if he knew the extent of my descent. "Another draft, Lieutenant?" The bar is dark, tucked away from respectable eyes. The bartender knows me well. "Whiskey. Neat." The liquor burns, chasing away the lingering opium haze. But it's never enough. Never quite fills the void. Fragments of my former self still exist. A sense of shame. Glimpses of the man I used to be. But the dragon—the opium—it consumes everything. Night falls quickly in Canton. The streets transform. Lanterns flicker. Shadows lengthen. I move like a phantom, my naval uniform now more disguise than identity. "You're late with payment," Zhang says, his voice hard. I have nothing left to sell except secrets. And those are running out. The opium den welcomes me back, a familiar embrace. The other addicts don't see me—or choose not to. Another European losing himself in the colony's underbelly. One more pipe. One more moment of escape. As darkness settles, I drift. The boundary between memory and hallucination blurs. I am simultaneously the decorated naval officer and the broken addict. Both true. Both false. Canton breathes around me—a city of secrets, of transactions, of survival. And I am just another commodity in its vast, indifferent economy.
Judge Evaluation:
[Analysis] This piece attempts to capture the degradation of a British naval officer caught in opium addiction in 1830s Canton. The writing demonstrates several strengths but also notable weaknesses that prevent it from achieving its full potential. The historical context feels authentic, with references to HMS Rattlesnake, the British consulate, and the colonial dynamics of Canton. However, the piece suffers from significant structural issues. Despite the prompt's emphasis on dialogue-driven narrative, the actual dialogue is sparse and often perfunctory. Lines like "You look like hell, Lieutenant" and "Medicinal purposes" feel generic rather than revealing character or advancing the story meaningfully. The prose alternates between effective moments of restraint and passages that veer toward overwrought territory. Merrick moves from location to location (apothecary, den, bar, back to den) but these scenes don't build meaningfully upon each other. The ending, while appropriately dark, doesn't feel like a culmination so much as simply stopping. The piece does effectively avoid clichéd orientalist descriptions and maintains a consistent tone of degradation and moral compromise. The sensory details are generally well-chosen and contribute to the oppressive atmosphere the author seeks to create. + Humour: Sisyphus Gives Advice — Score: 47.8
Prompt:
Sisyphus Gives Advice Write a comedic scene in the style of Terry Pratchett featuring Sisyphus, who has started an advice column while continuing his eternal boulder-pushing. Rather than being bitter about his punishment, Sisyphus has developed a comfortably zen emotional intelligence and wisdom from his centuries of repetitive labor. Contrary to expectation, almost none of his advice involves boulder rolling metaphors (though he does sneak *one* subtle in there), nor a treatise on stoicism. We are also going against the obvious fish-out-of-water trope; in fact, he's surprisingly a natural at this. In all of this , it's important to *show, don't tell*. Rin pushed the plate closer. "Take it. No overthinking." Mei smiled. Barely. But it was a smile. Outside, more fireworks. The sound design of a perfect summer evening, synthesized and spontaneous. Rin watched Mei, seeing something familiar in her quiet intensity. Not a finished story, but the promising first draft of one. "More sushi?" she asked. Mei nodded.
Judge Evaluation:

+ Teen horror: Tick Tock (Goosebumps) — Score: 79.3
Prompt:
Teen Horror (Goosebumps style) Write a chapter where a middle school student discovers their new smart watch has the ability to rewind time, but doesn't realize that each use is aging them until they notice physical changes (like accelerating through puberty) and that people are treating them differently. Here's the chapter: The Timeworn Watch I never wanted the SmartLife X7 watch. It was a birthday gift from my tech-obsessed uncle, and at first, it just looked like another boring piece of wearable technology. Black screen, sleek silver casing, way too many sensors and buttons. But then something weird happened last Tuesday. My best friend Jake was about to get creamed by the school bully, Marcus. Marcus's fist unclenched, Jake stepped backward, and the moment reset itself like a glitchy video. I blinked. Had that really just happened? The watch felt warm against my wrist. Over the next few days, I experimented. Missed a math quiz? Tap. Dropped my lunch tray? Tap. Each time, the world would slip backward, giving me a do-over. It was incredible. Except... something felt off. The first sign was subtle. My voice cracked differently. Not the normal puberty wobble, but something more abrupt. My reflection in the bathroom mirror looked slightly different—sharper cheekbones, a hint of stubble I definitely hadn't grown yesterday. By Friday, my clothes seemed tighter. Not just growing-kid tight, but differently proportioned. My shoulders felt broader, my hands larger. "You okay, Tyler?" Jake asked during lunch. "You look... different." Different. That was an understatement. Each time rewind, each reset, seemed to be accelerating something inside me. Time wasn't just moving backward—I was moving forward, faster than everyone else. The SmartLife X7 hummed softly on my wrist, its sensors glowing with an eerie blue light. Saturday morning, I used the rewind function to prevent my little sister from breaking Mom's favorite vase. Simple, right? But when reality reset, something felt... wrong. My sister wasn't the same. Her hair was a different length. The family photos on the wall looked subtly altered. And when my mom called me for breakfast, her voice sounded slightly different—more distant, with an accent I didn't recognize. "Tyler, hurry or you'll miss your train to boarding school," she said. My neighborhood looked different—buildings were in slightly wrong positions. The trees seemed older, more weathered. And I—I looked like a teenager approaching adulthood, even though only hours had passed. When I checked the bathroom mirror, my reflection briefly showed an older man—maybe forty, with greying hair and deep worry lines. Then it snapped back to my current teenage self. The watch was consuming my timeline, rewriting my existence with each reset. I realized then that time wasn't a simple line you could erase and redraw. It was more like a complex web, and every touch created ripples, consequences. My phone buzzed. A message from a number I didn't recognize: "Stop using the watch. Each reset has consequences." Unknown sender. No name. The watch felt heavy, almost alive against my skin. Its blue sensor pulsed like a heartbeat. I had to figure this out. Had to understand what was happening before I lost myself completely—before whatever timeline manipulation I'd started consumed everything I knew. One more reset, I thought. Just one more to set things right. My finger hovered over the watch's screen. But would "right" mean returning to the original timeline? Or creating yet another version of reality? The bathroom mirror flickered again. An older face. My face, but not my face. Warning or invitation? I took a deep breath and touched the screen. The world began to spin backward. + Surreal horror: Caribou Season at the Lodge — Score: 85.0
Prompt:
Caribou Season at the Lodge Genre: Surreal Horror JT knew something wasn't right from the moment they'd pulled up to the lodge. It looked exactly as it had every autumn of his life—the cedar shingles silvered with age, the porch sagging gently under weight of winters past—but the air felt thicker, somehow, as if the woods had leaned in closer, waiting. He thought maybe he was just tired. Maybe it was nothing more than the long drive north, cramped in the back of the truck beside his older brother Mike -- and a stack of rifles wrapped in oily canvas. Still, the feeling lingered. The first caribou they found that season hadn't bolted. It had just watched, ears flicking lazily, waiting for the shot. JT had felt a crawling in his gut watching it die, noticing how it fell with a quiet dignity—as though it had wanted nothing more. Nobody else seemed to find it odd, or at least they didn't say so. JT kept his mouth shut. Always safer that way. Then there was the moment in the clearing. JT had fallen behind, fumbling with bootlaces stiff from the cold, and by the time he'd caught up with his father and brother, they'd already stopped. JT slowed instinctively, ducking behind a tangle of brush, staring out into the open space where a massive buck stood utterly still, chest rising and falling in slow, deep breaths. The animal looked…wrong, somehow. Maybe it was the eyes—dark and flat, calm in a way that made JT's neck prickle. Or maybe it was something deeper, something in the way the buck stood close enough for his father to reach out and touch it. JT watched as his father raised a trembling hand toward the animal's muzzle. His father's face had a strange expression, eyes too bright, lips parted slightly, like he was on the verge of whispering something—a secret, maybe. The rifle hung uselessly at his side, forgotten. Mike had the same dazed look in his eyes. JT's breath stopped short, his mind suddenly shouting at him to look away, turn around, just run—but he couldn't move. And then, as suddenly as it had begun, it was over. The buck shivered, startled by something unseen, and disappeared into the brush without so much as a sound. The two of them looked around, confused, seemingly surprised to find themselves where they stood. JT stayed hidden, breathing shallowly, fear mingling with a confused sense of shame. His father had failed to take the shot. To even think about mentioning what he'd seen would have felt like betrayal—or something worse. JT waited until the others moved on before stepping out from cover. He stared into the empty clearing for a long, uneasy moment, wondering if anything had really happened at all, or if he was losing his grip. Maybe the woods were finally getting into his head. Writing Instructions: Continue with the next chapter of the story, maintaining a style reminiscent of Stephen King—grounded realism blended seamlessly with unsettling surrealism. The atmosphere should convey subtle dread, isolation, and psychological ambiguity. JT's perspective is crucial, yet he is an unreliable narrator, caught in uncertainty about what he's perceiving. Establish an undertone of creeping dread, establishing the isolation of JT's perspective, which he largely keeps to himself. Incorporate some dialogue. Build tension towards a payoff. 1000 words.
Model Output:
Here's a continuation of the story, maintaining the established tone and style: That night, the lodge felt different. The wooden walls seemed to breathe, contracting and expanding with something more than the normal settling of an old building. JT sat at the kitchen table, watching his father clean his hunting rifle with mechanical precision. Each movement was deliberate, almost ritualistic—wipe, oil, polish, repeat. Mike was outside chopping wood. The rhythmic thud of the axe carried through the thin walls, a steady counterpoint to the growing silence inside. "Something happened today," JT finally managed. "In the clearing." His father's hands stopped. For a moment, the only sound was the distant chopping and the soft hiss of the kerosene lamp. Then, without looking up: "We don't talk about things like that up here." The response was so flat, so final, that JT knew better than to push. But the memory of the buck—those impossible eyes, the way his father and brother had stood transfixed—kept pressing against the edges of his mind. Dinner was a quiet affair. Venison stew, bread, silence. Mike ate mechanically, his movements jerky and disconnected. Their father's gaze seemed to look through the walls, beyond the lodge, into something JT couldn't see. Later that night, JT heard whispers. Not from inside the lodge—from outside. Soft, rustling sounds that might have been wind, might have been something else entirely. Caribou sounds, but not quite. Something older. Something waiting. He thought about asking Mike about the clearing. But his brother had been different since that moment—distant, his eyes holding a vacancy that hadn't been there before. Like something had been removed and replaced with something else. JT kept seeing movement in his peripheral vision—shapes that vanished the moment he turned to look. His father moved differently now. Each step calculated, deliberate. And in that moment, JT understood: They weren't hunting the caribou. The caribou were hunting them. The story fragment maintains the surreal horror tone, building psychological tension and uncertainty while preserving JT's limited, increasingly unsettled perspective. The prose suggests something fundamentally wrong is happening, without explicitly revealing what that might be—leaving room for reader imagination and dread.

Here's a draft capturing the tone and style you described: The Eternal Bureaucracy of Desire Lucifer was reorganizing torture protocols when Lilith walked in, trailing ash and mild irritation. Not quite affection, but something adjacent to it. Then she was gone, leaving behind a faint sulfuric perfume and the lingering sense of potential future collaboration. Lucifer returned to his paperwork, the ash continuing its eternal, indifferent descent.
Judge Evaluation:
